					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Citrus fruit</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Orange peel" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Orange_peel_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Orange peel (disambiguation)">Orange peel (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Oranges_-_whole-halved-segment.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Oranges_-_whole-halved-segment.jpg/300px-Oranges_-_whole-halved-segment.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Oranges_-_whole-halved-segment.jpg/450px-Oranges_-_whole-halved-segment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Oranges_-_whole-halved-segment.jpg/600px-Oranges_-_whole-halved-segment.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a><figcaption>Orange—whole, halved, and peeled segment</figcaption></figure>
<p>An <b>orange</b>, also called <b>sweet orange</b> to distinguish it from the <a href="/wiki/Bitter_orange" title="Bitter orange">bitter orange</a> (<i>Citrus × aurantium</i>), is the <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> of a tree in the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Rutaceae" title="Rutaceae">Rutaceae</a>. Botanically, this is the hybrid <a href="/wiki/Citrus_%C3%97_sinensis" title="Citrus × sinensis"><i>Citrus</i> × <i>sinensis</i></a>, between the <a href="/wiki/Pomelo" title="Pomelo">pomelo</a> (<i>Citrus maxima</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_orange" title="Mandarin orange">mandarin orange</a> (<i>Citrus reticulata</i>). The <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplast</a> <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>, and therefore the maternal line, is that of pomelo. The sweet orange has had its full <a href="/wiki/Whole_genome_sequencing" title="Whole genome sequencing">genome sequenced</a>.
</p><p>The orange originated in a region encompassing <a href="/wiki/Southern_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern China">Southern China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northeast_India" title="Northeast India">Northeast India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>; the earliest mention of the sweet orange was in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_literature" title="Chinese literature">Chinese literature</a> in 314 BC. Orange trees are widely grown for their sweet fruit. The fruit of the <a href="/wiki/Orange_tree" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange tree">orange tree</a> can be eaten fresh, or processed for its juice or fragrant <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">peel</a>. In 2022, 76 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of oranges were grown worldwide, with <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> producing 22% of the total, followed by <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.
</p><p>Oranges have featured in human culture since ancient times. They first appear in Western art in the <i><a href="/wiki/Arnolfini_Portrait" title="Arnolfini Portrait">Arnolfini Portrait</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Eyck" title="Jan van Eyck">Jan van Eyck</a>, but they had been depicted in Chinese art centuries earlier, as in Zhao Lingrang's <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> fan painting <i>Yellow Oranges and Green Tangerines</i>. By the 17th century, an orangery had become an item of prestige in Europe, as seen at the <a href="/wiki/Versailles_Orangerie" title="Versailles Orangerie">Versailles Orangerie</a>. More recently, artists such as <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Sloan" title="John Sloan">John Sloan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a> included oranges in their paintings.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<p>The orange tree is a relatively small <a href="/wiki/Evergreen" title="Evergreen">evergreen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering</a> tree, with an average height of 9 to 10&#160;m (30 to 33&#160;ft), although some very old specimens can reach 15&#160;m (49&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-webber4_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webber4-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Its oval <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaves</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Leaf_arrangement" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaf arrangement">alternately arranged</a>, are 4 to 10&#160;cm (1.6 to 3.9&#160;in) long and have <a href="/wiki/Crenulate" class="mw-redirect" title="Crenulate">crenulate</a> margins.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Sweet oranges grow in a range of different sizes, and shapes varying from spherical to oblong. Inside and attached to the rind is a porous white tissue, the white, bitter <a href="/wiki/Mesocarp" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesocarp">mesocarp</a> or albedo (<i><a href="/wiki/Pith" title="Pith">pith</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The orange contains a number of distinct <i><a href="/wiki/Carpel" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpel">carpels</a></i> (segments or pigs, botanically the fruits) inside, typically about ten, each delimited by a membrane and containing many <a href="/wiki/Juice_vesicles" title="Juice vesicles">juice-filled vesicles</a> and usually a few <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">pips</a>. When unripe, the fruit is green. The grainy irregular rind of the ripe fruit can range from bright orange to yellow-orange, but frequently retains green patches or, under warm climate conditions, remains entirely green. Like all other citrus fruits, the sweet orange is non-<a href="/wiki/Climacteric_(botany)" title="Climacteric (botany)">climacteric</a>, not ripening off the tree. The <i>Citrus sinensis</i> group is subdivided into four classes with distinct characteristics: common oranges, blood or pigmented oranges, navel oranges, and acidless oranges.<sup id="cite_ref-Kimball_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kimball-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Webber1_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webber1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sauls_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sauls-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The fruit is a <a href="/wiki/Hesperidium" title="Hesperidium">hesperidium</a>, a modified <a href="/wiki/Berry_(botany)" title="Berry (botany)">berry</a>; it is covered by a <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">rind</a> formed by a rugged thickening of the <a href="/wiki/Fruit_anatomy#Pericarp_layers" class="mw-redirect" title="Fruit anatomy">ovary wall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bailey_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_origins">Hybrid origins</span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">Citrus</a></i> trees are <a href="/wiki/Angiosperms" class="mw-redirect" title="Angiosperms">angiosperms</a>, and most species are almost entirely <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Hybrid plant">interfertile</a>. This includes <a href="/wiki/Grapefruit" title="Grapefruit">grapefruits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lemon" title="Lemon">lemons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lime_(fruit)" title="Lime (fruit)">limes</a>, oranges, and many <a href="/wiki/Citrus_hybrids" class="mw-redirect" title="Citrus hybrids">citrus hybrids</a>. As the interfertility of oranges and other citrus has produced numerous hybrids and <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sport_(botany)" title="Sport (botany)">bud mutations</a> have also been selected, <a href="/wiki/Citrus_taxonomy" title="Citrus taxonomy">citrus taxonomy</a> has proven difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-Nicolosi_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicolosi-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sweet orange, <i><a href="/wiki/Citrus_%C3%97_sinensis" title="Citrus × sinensis">Citrus x sinensis</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-USDA_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDA-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> is not a wild fruit, but arose in <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> in East Asia. It originated in a region encompassing <a href="/wiki/Southern_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern China">Southern China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northeast_India" title="Northeast India">Northeast India</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
The fruit was created as a cross between a non-pure <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_orange" title="Mandarin orange">mandarin orange</a> and a hybrid <a href="/wiki/Pomelo" title="Pomelo">pomelo</a> that had a substantial mandarin component.<sup id="cite_ref-fullgenome_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fullgenome-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AGLthesis_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AGLthesis-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Since its <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast_DNA" title="Chloroplast DNA">chloroplast DNA</a> is that of pomelo, it was likely the hybrid pomelo, perhaps a pomelo <a href="/wiki/Backcrossing" title="Backcrossing">BC1 backcross</a>, that was the maternal parent of the first orange.<sup id="cite_ref-genealogy_review_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-genealogy_review-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wu_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wu-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Based on genomic analysis, the relative proportions of the ancestral species in the sweet orange are approximately 42% pomelo and 58% mandarin.<sup id="cite_ref-Wu_Terol_Ibanez_2018_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wu_Terol_Ibanez_2018-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> All varieties of the sweet orange descend from this prototype cross, differing only by mutations selected for during agricultural propagation.<sup id="cite_ref-Wu_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wu-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Sweet oranges have a distinct origin from the bitter orange, which arose independently, perhaps in the wild, from a cross between pure mandarin and pomelo parents.<sup id="cite_ref-Wu_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wu-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sweet oranges have in turn given rise to many further hybrids including the <a href="/wiki/Grapefruit" title="Grapefruit">grapefruit</a>, which arose from a sweet orange x pomelo backcross. Spontaneous and engineered backcrosses between the sweet orange and mandarin oranges or tangerines have produced the <a href="/wiki/Clementine" title="Clementine">clementine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Murcott_(fruit)" title="Murcott (fruit)">murcott</a>. The ambersweet is a complex sweet orange x (Orlando <a href="/wiki/Tangelo" title="Tangelo">tangelo</a> x clementine) hybrid.<sup id="cite_ref-Wu_Terol_Ibanez_2018_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wu_Terol_Ibanez_2018-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Citrange" title="Citrange">citranges</a> are a group of sweet orange x <a href="/wiki/Trifoliate_orange" title="Trifoliate orange">trifoliate orange</a> (<i>Citrus trifoliata</i>) hybrids.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hybrid_origins_of_orange.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Hybrid_origins_of_orange.svg/440px-Hybrid_origins_of_orange.svg.png" decoding="async" width="440" height="330" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Hybrid_origins_of_orange.svg/660px-Hybrid_origins_of_orange.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Hybrid_origins_of_orange.svg/880px-Hybrid_origins_of_orange.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a><figcaption>The orange is a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_orange" title="Mandarin orange">mandarin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pomelo" title="Pomelo">pomelo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wu_Terol_Ibanez_2018_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wu_Terol_Ibanez_2018-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_Agricultural_Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bayad-wa-riyad%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-%D9%88-%D8%B1%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Bayad-wa-riyad%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-%D9%88-%D8%B1%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-.jpg/170px-Bayad-wa-riyad%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-%D9%88-%D8%B1%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="206" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Bayad-wa-riyad%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-%D9%88-%D8%B1%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-.jpg/255px-Bayad-wa-riyad%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-%D9%88-%D8%B1%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Bayad-wa-riyad%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-%D9%88-%D8%B1%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-.jpg/340px-Bayad-wa-riyad%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-%D9%88-%D8%B1%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%B6-.jpg 2x" data-file-width="477" data-file-height="579" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a> spread citrus fruits as far as the Iberian Peninsula. Page from the <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith_Bayad_wa_Riyad" title="Hadith Bayad wa Riyad">Hadith Bayad wa Riyad</a></i>, 13th century</figcaption></figure>
<p>In Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> introduced citrus fruits including the bitter orange, lemon, and lime to <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_1974_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_1974-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Large-scale cultivation started in the 10th century, as evidenced by complex irrigation techniques specifically adapted to support orange orchards.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_1974_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_1974-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Citrus fruits—among them the bitter orange—were introduced to Sicily in the 9th century during the period of the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Sicily" title="Emirate of Sicily">Emirate of Sicily</a>, but the sweet orange was unknown there until the late 15th century or the beginnings of the 16th century, when Italian and Portuguese merchants brought orange trees into the Mediterranean area.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_across_Europe">Spread across Europe</span></h3>
<p>Shortly afterward, the sweet orange quickly was adopted as an edible fruit. It was considered a luxury food grown by wealthy people in private conservatories, called <a href="/wiki/Orangerie" class="mw-redirect" title="Orangerie">orangeries</a>. By 1646, the sweet orange was well known throughout Europe; it went on to become the most often cultivated of all fruit trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> of France had a great love of orange trees and built the grandest of all royal <a href="/wiki/Orangerie_of_Versailles" class="mw-redirect" title="Orangerie of Versailles">Orangeries</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Palace of Versailles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> At Versailles, potted orange trees in solid silver tubs were placed throughout the rooms of the palace, while the Orangerie allowed year-round cultivation of the fruit to supply the court. When Louis condemned his finance minister, <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Fouquet" title="Nicolas Fouquet">Nicolas Fouquet</a>, in 1664, part of the treasures that he confiscated were over 1,000 orange trees from Fouquet's estate at <a href="/wiki/Vaux-le-Vicomte" title="Vaux-le-Vicomte">Vaux-le-Vicomte</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="To_the_Americas">To the Americas</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a></div>
<p>Spanish travelers introduced the sweet orange to the American continent. On his second voyage in 1493, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> may have planted the fruit on <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sauls_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sauls-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Subsequent expeditions in the mid-1500s brought sweet oranges to South America and Mexico, and to Florida in 1565, when <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Men%C3%A9ndez_de_Avil%C3%A9s" title="Pedro Menéndez de Avilés">Pedro Menéndez de Avilés</a> founded <a href="/wiki/St._Augustine,_Florida" title="St. Augustine, Florida">St Augustine</a>. <a href="/wiki/Spanish_missions_in_Arizona" title="Spanish missions in Arizona">Spanish missionaries</a> brought orange trees to Arizona between 1707 and 1710, while the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_missions_in_California" title="Spanish missions in California">Franciscans</a> did the same in San Diego, California, in 1769.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Menzies" title="Archibald Menzies">Archibald Menzies</a>, the botanist on the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Expedition" title="Vancouver Expedition">Vancouver Expedition</a>, collected orange seeds in South Africa, raised the seedlings onboard, and gave them to several Hawaiian chiefs in 1792. The sweet orange came to be grown across the <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaiian Islands</a>, but its cultivation stopped after the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Ceratitis_capitata" title="Ceratitis capitata">Mediterranean fruit fly</a> in the early 1900s.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> farmers obtained seeds from New Orleans around 1872, after which orange groves were established by grafting the sweet orange on to sour orange rootstocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orange_(word)" title="Orange (word)"><i>Orange</i> (word)</a></div>
<p>The word "orange" derives ultimately from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Dravidian" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Dravidian">Proto-Dravidian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a> <span title="Tamil-language text"><span lang="ta">நாரம்</span></span> (<span title="Tamil-language romanization"><i lang="ta-Latn">nāram</i></span>). From there the word entered <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <span title="Sanskrit-language text"><span lang="sa">नारङ्ग</span></span> (<span title="Sanskrit-language romanization"><i lang="sa-Latn">nāraṅga</i></span>), meaning 'orange tree'. The Sanskrit word reached <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">European languages</a> through <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> <span title="Persian-language text"><span lang="fa" dir="rtl">نارنگ</span></span> (<span title="Persian-language romanization"><i lang="fa-Latn">nārang</i></span>) and its <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> derivative <span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl">نارنج</span></span> (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">nāranj</i></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-Online_Etym_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Online_Etym-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The word entered <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Middle English">Late Middle English</a> in the 14th century via <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <span title="Old French (842-ca. 1400)-language text"><i lang="fro">pomme d'orenge</i></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Other forms include <a href="/wiki/Old_Proven%C3%A7al" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Provençal">Old Provençal</a> <span title="Old Provençal (to 1500)-language text"><i lang="pro">auranja</i></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-Collins_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Italian <i>arancia</i>, formerly <i>narancia</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Online_Etym_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Online_Etym-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In several languages, the initial <i>n</i> present in earlier forms of the word dropped off because it may have been mistaken as part of an indefinite article ending in an <i>n</i> sound. In French, for example, <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">une norenge</i></span> may have been heard as <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">une orenge</i></span>. This linguistic change is called <a href="/wiki/Juncture_loss" class="mw-redirect" title="Juncture loss">juncture loss</a>. <a href="/wiki/Orange_(color)" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange (color)">The color</a> was named after the fruit,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> with the first recorded use of <i>orange</i> as a color name in English in 1512.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span></h2>

<p>Orange flesh is 87% water, 12% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 1% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and contains negligible <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> (see table). As a 100 gram reference amount, orange flesh provides 47 <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calories</a>, and is a rich source of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a>, providing 64% of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a>. No other <a href="/wiki/Essential_nutrient" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential nutrient">micronutrients</a> are present in significant amounts (see table).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phytochemicals">Phytochemicals</span></h3>
<p>Oranges contain diverse <a href="/wiki/Phytochemical" title="Phytochemical">phytochemicals</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> (<a href="/wiki/Beta-carotene" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta-carotene">beta-carotene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lutein" title="Lutein">lutein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta-cryptoxanthin" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta-cryptoxanthin">beta-cryptoxanthin</a>), <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Naringenin" title="Naringenin">naringenin</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and numerous <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Volatile organic compounds">volatile organic compounds</a> producing orange <a href="/wiki/Odor" title="Odor">aroma</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry)" title="Alcohol (chemistry)">alcohols</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Orange juice contains only about one-fifth the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric acid</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lime_(fruit)" title="Lime (fruit)">lime</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lemon" title="Lemon">lemon</a> juice (which contain about 47 g/L).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taste">Taste</span></h3>
<p>The taste of oranges is determined mainly by the ratio of sugars to acids, whereas orange aroma derives from <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">volatile organic compounds</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry)" title="Alcohol (chemistry)">alcohols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">terpenes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tietel_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tietel-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hadi_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hadi-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Bitter <a href="/wiki/Limonoid" title="Limonoid">limonoid</a> compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Limonin" title="Limonin">limonin</a>, decrease gradually during development, whereas volatile aroma compounds tend to peak in mid– to late–season development.<sup id="cite_ref-bai_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bai-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Taste quality tends to improve later in harvests when there is a higher sugar/acid ratio with less bitterness.<sup id="cite_ref-bai_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bai-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> As a citrus fruit, the orange is acidic, with <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> levels ranging from 2.9<sup id="cite_ref-sinclair_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinclair-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> to 4.0.<sup id="cite_ref-sinclair_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinclair-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Taste and aroma vary according to genetic background, environmental conditions during development, ripeness at harvest, postharvest conditions, and storage duration.<sup id="cite_ref-tietel_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tietel-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hadi_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hadi-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Octyl_acetate.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Octyl_acetate.svg/170px-Octyl_acetate.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="46" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Octyl_acetate.svg/255px-Octyl_acetate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Octyl_acetate.svg/340px-Octyl_acetate.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="632" data-file-height="172" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Octyl_acetate" title="Octyl acetate">Octyl acetate</a>, a volatile compound contributing to the fragrance of oranges</figcaption></figure>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivars">Cultivars</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common">Common</span></h3>
<p>Common oranges (also called "white", "round", or "blond" oranges) constitute about two-thirds of all orange production. The majority of this crop is used for juice.<sup id="cite_ref-Kimball_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kimball-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sauls_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sauls-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Valencia">Valencia</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Valencia_orange" title="Valencia orange">Valencia orange</a></div>
<p>The Valencia orange is a late-season fruit; it is popular when navel oranges are out of season. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Rivers_(nurseryman)" title="Thomas Rivers (nurseryman)">Thomas Rivers</a>, an English nurseryman, imported this variety from the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a> and catalogued it in 1865 under the name Excelsior. Around 1870, he provided trees to S. B. Parsons, a <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a> nurseryman, who in turn sold them to E. H. Hart of <a href="/wiki/Federal_Point,_Florida" title="Federal Point, Florida">Federal Point, Florida</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-coit_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coit-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navel">Navel</span></h3>
<p>Navel oranges have a characteristic second fruit at the <a href="/wiki/Apical_meristems" class="mw-redirect" title="Apical meristems">apex</a>, which protrudes slightly like a human <a href="/wiki/Navel" title="Navel">navel</a>. They are mainly an eating fruit, as their thicker skin makes them easy to peel, they are less juicy and their bitterness makes them less suitable for juice.<sup id="cite_ref-Kimball_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kimball-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The parent variety was probably the Portuguese navel orange or <i>Umbigo</i> described by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Risso" title="Antoine Risso">Antoine Risso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Antoine_Poiteau" title="Pierre Antoine Poiteau">Pierre Antoine Poiteau</a> in their 1818–1822 book <i>Histoire naturelle des orangers</i> ("Natural History of Orange Trees").<sup id="cite_ref-Washington_on_Citrus_ID_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Washington_on_Citrus_ID-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The mutation caused the orange to develop a second fruit at its base, opposite the stem, embedded within the peel of the primary orange. Navel oranges were introduced in Australia in 1824 and in Florida in 1835. In 1873, <a href="/wiki/Eliza_Tibbets" title="Eliza Tibbets">Eliza Tibbets</a> planted two cuttings of the original tree in <a href="/wiki/Riverside,_California" title="Riverside, California">Riverside, California</a>, where the fruit became known as "Washington".<sup id="cite_ref-Washington_on_Citrus_ID_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Washington_on_Citrus_ID-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The cultivar rapidly spread to other countries, but being seedless it had to be propagated by <a href="/wiki/Cutting_(plant)" title="Cutting (plant)">cutting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grafting" title="Grafting">grafting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cfaitc_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cfaitc-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cara_cara_orange" class="mw-redirect" title="Cara cara orange">Cara cara orange</a> is a type of navel orange grown mainly in <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> and California's <a href="/wiki/San_Joaquin_Valley" title="San Joaquin Valley">San Joaquin Valley</a>. It is sweet and low in acid,<sup id="cite_ref-UBC_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UBC-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> with distinctively pinkish red flesh. It was discovered at the <a href="/wiki/Hacienda" title="Hacienda">Hacienda</a> Cara Cara in <a href="/wiki/Valencia,_Venezuela" title="Valencia, Venezuela">Valencia</a>, Venezuela, in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-UCR_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCR-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood">Blood</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blood_orange" title="Blood orange">Blood orange</a></div>
<p>Blood oranges, with an intense red coloration inside, are widely grown around the Mediterranean; there are several cultivars.<sup id="cite_ref-Morton_1987_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton_1987-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The development of the red color requires cool nights.<sup id="cite_ref-McGee_2004_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGee_2004-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The redness is mainly due to the <a href="/wiki/Anthocyanin" title="Anthocyanin">anthocyanin</a> pigment <a href="/wiki/Chrysanthemin" title="Chrysanthemin">chrysanthemin</a> (cyanidin 3-<i>O</i>-glucoside).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acidless">Acidless</span></h3>
<p>Acidless oranges are an early-season fruit with very low levels of acid. They also are called "sweet" oranges in the United States, with similar names in other countries: <i>douce</i> in France, <i>sucrena</i> in Spain, <i>dolce</i> or <i>maltese</i> in Italy, <i>meski</i> in North Africa and the Near East (where they are especially popular), <i>succari</i> in Egypt, and <i>lima</i> in Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-Kimball_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kimball-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The lack of acid, which protects orange juice against spoilage in other groups, renders them generally unfit for processing as juice, so they are primarily eaten. They remain profitable in areas of local consumption, but rapid spoilage renders them unsuitable for export to major population centres of Europe, Asia, or the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Kimball_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kimball-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 234.66666666667px">

		</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<p>Like most citrus plants, oranges do well under moderate temperatures—between 15.5 and 29&#160;°C (59.9 and 84.2&#160;°F)—and require considerable amounts of sunshine and water. As oranges are sensitive to <a href="/wiki/Frost" title="Frost">frost</a>, farmers have developed methods to protect the trees from frost damage. A common process is to spray the trees with water so as to cover them with a thin layer of ice, insulating them even if air temperatures drop far lower. This practice, however, offers protection only for a very short time.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Another procedure involves burning fuel oil in <a href="/wiki/Smudge_pot" title="Smudge pot">smudge pots</a> put between the trees. These burn with a great deal of particulate emission, so condensation of water vapour on the particulate soot prevents condensation on plants and raises the air temperature very slightly. Smudge pots were developed after a disastrous freeze in Southern California in January 1913 destroyed a whole crop.<sup id="cite_ref-moore_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moore-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propagation">Propagation</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fruit_tree_propagation" title="Fruit tree propagation">Fruit tree propagation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Citrus_rootstock" title="Citrus rootstock">Citrus rootstock</a></div>
<p>Commercially grown orange trees are <a href="/wiki/Plant_propagation" title="Plant propagation">propagated</a> <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexually</a> by <a href="/wiki/Grafting" title="Grafting">grafting</a> a mature <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a> onto a suitable <a href="/wiki/Seedling" title="Seedling">seedling</a> <a href="/wiki/Rootstock" title="Rootstock">rootstock</a> to ensure the same <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">yield</a>, identical fruit characteristics, and resistance to diseases throughout the years. Propagation involves two stages: first, a rootstock is grown from seed. Then, when it is approximately one year old, the leafy top is cut off and a <a href="/wiki/Bud" title="Bud">bud</a> taken from a specific <a href="/wiki/Scion_(grafting)" class="mw-redirect" title="Scion (grafting)">scion</a> variety, is grafted into its bark. The scion is what determines the variety of orange, while the rootstock makes the tree resistant to pests and diseases and adaptable to specific <a href="/wiki/Soil_type" title="Soil type">soil</a> and climatic conditions. Thus, rootstocks influence the rate of growth and have an effect on fruit yield and quality.<sup id="cite_ref-Lacey_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lacey-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Rootstocks must be compatible with the variety inserted into them because otherwise, the tree may decline, be less productive, or die.<sup id="cite_ref-Lacey_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lacey-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Among the advantages to grafting are that trees mature uniformly and begin to bear fruit earlier than those reproduced by seeds (3 to 4 years in contrast with 6 to 7 years),<sup id="cite_ref-Citrus_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Citrus-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and that farmers can combine the best attributes of a scion with those of a rootstock.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harvest">Harvest</span></h3>
<p>Canopy-shaking mechanical harvesters are being used increasingly in Florida to harvest oranges. Current canopy shaker machines use a series of six-to-seven-foot-long tines to shake the tree canopy at a relatively constant  stroke and frequency.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Oranges are picked once they are pale orange.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Degreening">Degreening</span></h3>
<p>Oranges must be mature when harvested. In the United States, laws forbid harvesting immature fruit for human consumption in Texas, Arizona, California and Florida.<sup id="cite_ref-Wagner_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wagner-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Ripe oranges, however, often have some green or yellow-green color in the skin. <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">Ethylene</a> gas is used to turn green skin to orange. This process is known as "degreening", "gassing", "sweating", or "curing".<sup id="cite_ref-Wagner_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wagner-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Oranges are non-<a href="/wiki/Climacteric_(botany)" title="Climacteric (botany)">climacteric</a> fruits and cannot ripen internally in response to ethylene gas after harvesting, though they will de-green externally.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage">Storage</span></h3>
<p>Commercially, oranges can be stored by refrigeration in controlled-atmosphere chambers for up to twelve weeks after harvest. Storage life ultimately depends on cultivar, maturity, pre-harvest conditions, and handling.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> At home, oranges have a shelf life of about one month, and are best stored loose.<sup id="cite_ref-cpma_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cpma-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests_and_diseases">Pests and diseases</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests">Pests</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Icerya_purchasi_1435060.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Icerya_purchasi_1435060.jpg/220px-Icerya_purchasi_1435060.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Icerya_purchasi_1435060.jpg/330px-Icerya_purchasi_1435060.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Icerya_purchasi_1435060.jpg/440px-Icerya_purchasi_1435060.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Cottony_cushion_scale" class="mw-redirect" title="Cottony cushion scale">Cottony cushion scale</a> insects devastated orange groves across California in the 19th century, and were the first pest to be subject to successful <a href="/wiki/Biological_control" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological control">biological control</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-coit_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coit-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>The first major pest that attacked orange trees in the United States was the cottony cushion scale (<i><a href="/wiki/Icerya_purchasi" title="Icerya purchasi">Icerya purchasi</a></i>), imported from Australia to California in 1868. Within 20 years, it wiped out the citrus orchards around Los Angeles, and limited orange growth throughout California. In 1888, the USDA sent Alfred Koebele to Australia to study this <a href="/wiki/Scale_insect" title="Scale insect">scale insect</a> in its native habitat. He brought back with him specimens of an Australian <a href="/wiki/Coccinellidae" title="Coccinellidae">ladybird</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Novius_cardinalis" title="Novius cardinalis">Novius cardinalis</a></i> (the Vedalia beetle), and within a decade the pest was controlled. This was one of the first successful applications of <a href="/wiki/Biological_pest_control" title="Biological pest control">biological pest control</a> on any crop.<sup id="cite_ref-coit_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coit-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Orange_dog" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange dog">orange dog</a> caterpillar of the giant swallowtail butterfly, <i>Papilio cresphontes</i>, is a pest of citrus plantations in North America, where it eats new foliage and can defoliate young trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Giant_Swallowtail_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giant_Swallowtail-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_citrus_diseases" title="List of citrus diseases">List of citrus diseases</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Asian_Citrus_Psyllid_adult.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Asian_Citrus_Psyllid_adult.jpg/220px-Asian_Citrus_Psyllid_adult.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="140" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Asian_Citrus_Psyllid_adult.jpg/330px-Asian_Citrus_Psyllid_adult.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Asian_Citrus_Psyllid_adult.jpg/440px-Asian_Citrus_Psyllid_adult.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1428" data-file-height="908" /></a><figcaption>The Asian citrus psyllid, <i><a href="/wiki/Diaphorina_citri" title="Diaphorina citri">Diaphorina citri</a></i>, is a major <a href="/wiki/Disease_vector" title="Disease vector">vector</a> of <a href="/wiki/Citrus_greening_disease" title="Citrus greening disease">citrus greening disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Killiny_Nehela_2021_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Killiny_Nehela_2021-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Citrus_greening_disease" title="Citrus greening disease">Citrus greening disease</a>, caused by the bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Liberobacter_asiaticum" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberobacter asiaticum">Liberobacter asiaticum</a></i>, has been the most serious threat to orange production since 2010. It is characterized by streaks of different shades on the leaves, and deformed, poorly colored, unsavory fruit. In areas where the disease is endemic, citrus trees live for only five to eight years and never bear fruit suitable for consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-halbert_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halbert-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In the western hemisphere, the disease was discovered in Florida in 1998, where it has attacked nearly all the trees ever since. It was reported in Brazil by Fundecitrus Brasil in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-halbert_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halbert-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> As from 2009, 0.87% of the trees in Brazil's main orange growing areas (São Paulo and Minas Gerais) showed symptoms of greening, an increase of 49% over 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-gainbr9006_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gainbr9006-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> 
The disease is spread primarily by <a href="/wiki/Psyllid" title="Psyllid">psyllid</a> plant lice such as the Asian citrus psyllid (<i><a href="/wiki/Diaphorina_citri" title="Diaphorina citri">Diaphorina citri</a></i> Kuwayama), an efficient <a href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vector</a> of the bacterium.<sup id="cite_ref-Killiny_Nehela_2021_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Killiny_Nehela_2021-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Foliar insecticides reduce psyllid populations for a short time, but also suppress beneficial predatory ladybird beetles. Soil application of <a href="/wiki/Aldicarb" title="Aldicarb">aldicarb</a> provided limited control of Asian citrus psyllid, while drenches of <a href="/wiki/Imidacloprid" title="Imidacloprid">imidacloprid</a> to young trees were effective for two months or more.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Management of citrus greening disease requires an integrated approach that includes use of clean stock, elimination of inoculum via voluntary and regulatory means, use of pesticides to control psyllid vectors in the citrus crop, and biological control of the vectors in non-crop reservoirs.<sup id="cite_ref-halbert_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halbert-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Greasy spot, a <a href="/wiki/Fungal_infection_in_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungal infection in plants">fungal disease</a> caused by the ascomycete <i><a href="/wiki/Mycosphaerella_citri" title="Mycosphaerella citri">Mycosphaerella citri</a></i>, produces leaf spots and premature defoliation, thus reducing the tree's vigour and yield. <a href="/wiki/Ascospore" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascospore">Ascospores</a> of <i>M. citri</i> are generated in <a href="/wiki/Ascocarp" title="Ascocarp">pseudothecia</a> in decomposing fallen leaves.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Citrus_production" title="Citrus production">Citrus production</a></div>
<p>In 2022, world production of oranges was 76 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> with 22% of the total, followed by India, China, and Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-faostat_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> has established <a href="/wiki/Food_grading" title="Food grading">grades</a> for Florida oranges, primarily for oranges sold as fresh fruit.<sup id="cite_ref-usda_fla_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usda_fla-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In the United States, groves are located mainly in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The majority of California's crop is sold as fresh fruit, whereas Florida's oranges are destined to juice products. The <a href="/wiki/Indian_River_(Florida)" title="Indian River (Florida)">Indian River</a> area of Florida produces high quality juice, which is often sold fresh and blended with juice from other regions, because Indian River trees yield sweet oranges but in relatively small quantities.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culinary_use">Culinary use</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dessert_fruit_and_juice">Dessert fruit and juice</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Orange_juice" title="Orange juice">Orange juice</a></div>
<p>Oranges, whose flavor may vary from <a href="/wiki/Sweet" class="mw-redirect" title="Sweet">sweet</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sour" class="mw-redirect" title="Sour">sour</a>, are commonly peeled and eaten fresh raw as a dessert. <a href="/wiki/Orange_juice" title="Orange juice">Orange juice</a> is obtained by squeezing the fruit on a special tool (a <i>juicer</i> or <i>squeezer</i>) and collecting the juice in a tray or tank underneath. This can be made at home or, on a much larger scale, industrially.<sup id="cite_ref-juice_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-juice-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Orange juice is a traded commodity on the <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_Exchange" title="Intercontinental Exchange">Intercontinental Exchange</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lawson_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lawson-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Frozen orange juice concentrate is made from freshly squeezed and filtered juice.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marmalade">Marmalade</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marmalade" title="Marmalade">Marmalade</a></div>
<p>Oranges are made into <a href="/wiki/Fruit_preserves" title="Fruit preserves">jam</a> in many countries; in Britain, bitter <a href="/wiki/Seville_orange" class="mw-redirect" title="Seville orange">Seville oranges</a> are used to make <a href="/wiki/Marmalade" title="Marmalade">marmalade</a>. Almost the whole Spanish production is exported to Britain for this purpose. The entire fruit is cut up and boiled with sugar; the pith contributes <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a>, which helps the marmalade to set. The first recipe was by an Englishwoman, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Kettilby" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary Kettilby">Mary Kettilby</a>, in 1714. Pieces of peel were first added by <a href="/wiki/Janet_Keiller" class="mw-redirect" title="Janet Keiller">Janet Keiller</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dundee" title="Dundee">Dundee</a> in the 1790s, contributing a distinctively bitter taste.<sup id="cite_ref-Bateman_1993_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bateman_1993-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Orange peel contains the bitter substances <a href="/wiki/Limonene" title="Limonene">limonene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naringin" title="Naringin">naringin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hasegawa_Berhow_Fong_1996_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasegawa_Berhow_Fong_1996-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extracts">Extracts</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Limonene" title="Limonene">Limonene</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Zest_(ingredient)" title="Zest (ingredient)">Zest</a> is scraped from the <a href="/wiki/Flavedo" class="mw-redirect" title="Flavedo">coloured outer part of the peel</a>, and used as a flavoring and garnish in desserts and <a href="/wiki/Cocktail" title="Cocktail">cocktails</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sweet <a href="/wiki/Orange_oil" title="Orange oil">orange oil</a> is a <a href="/wiki/By-product" title="By-product">by-product</a> of the juice industry produced by pressing the peel. It is used for flavoring food and drinks; it is employed in the perfume industry and in <a href="/wiki/Aromatherapy" title="Aromatherapy">aromatherapy</a> for its <a href="/wiki/Fragrance" class="mw-redirect" title="Fragrance">fragrance</a>. The oil consists of approximately 90% <small>D</small>-limonene, a <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> used in household chemicals such as wood conditioners for furniture and—along with other citrus oils—detergents and hand cleansers. It is an efficient cleaning agent with a pleasant smell, promoted for being environmentally friendly and therefore, preferable to petrochemicals. It is, however, irritating to the skin and toxic to aquatic life.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-nolines center">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Oranges_and_orange_juice.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Fruit and juice"><img alt="Fruit and juice" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Oranges_and_orange_juice.jpg/131px-Oranges_and_orange_juice.jpg" decoding="async" width="131" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Oranges_and_orange_juice.jpg/197px-Oranges_and_orange_juice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Oranges_and_orange_juice.jpg/262px-Oranges_and_orange_juice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1772" data-file-height="2700" /></a></span></div>

		</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_human_culture">In human culture</span></h2>
<p>Oranges have featured in human culture since ancient times. The earliest mention of the sweet orange in Chinese literature dates from 314 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-fullgenome_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fullgenome-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Larissa Pham, in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Paris_Review" title="The Paris Review">The Paris Review</a></i>, notes that sweet oranges were available in China much earlier than in the West. She writes that Zhao Lingrang's fan painting <i>Yellow Oranges and Green Tangerines</i> pays attention not to the fruit's colour but the shape of the fruit-laden trees, and that Su Shi's poem on the same subject runs "You must remember, / the best scenery of the year, / Is exactly now, / when oranges turn yellow and tangerines green."<sup id="cite_ref-Pham_2019_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pham_2019-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The scholar Cristina Mazzoni has examined the multiple uses of the fruit in Italian art and literature, from <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Siena" title="Catherine of Siena">Catherine of Siena</a>'s sending of candied oranges to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_V" title="Pope Urban V">Pope Urban</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Sandro_Botticelli" title="Sandro Botticelli">Sandro Botticelli</a>'s setting of his painting <i><a href="/wiki/Primavera_(Botticelli)" title="Primavera (Botticelli)">Primavera</a></i> in an orange grove. She notes that oranges symbolised desire and wealth on the one hand, and deformity on the other, while in the fairy-stories of Sicily, they have magical properties.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Pham comments that the <i><a href="/wiki/Arnolfini_Portrait" title="Arnolfini Portrait">Arnolfini Portrait</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Eyck" title="Jan van Eyck">Jan van Eyck</a> contains in a small detail one of the first representations of oranges in Western art, the costly fruit perhaps traded by the merchant Arnolfini himself.<sup id="cite_ref-Pham_2019_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pham_2019-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> By the 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Orangery" title="Orangery">orangeries</a> were added to great houses in Europe, both to enable the fruit to be grown locally and for prestige, as seen in the <a href="/wiki/Versailles_Orangerie" title="Versailles Orangerie">Versailles Orangerie</a> completed in 1686.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The Dutch <a href="/wiki/Post-Impressionism" title="Post-Impressionism">post-impressionist</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a> portrayed oranges in paintings such as his 1889 <i>Still Life of Oranges and Lemons with Blue Gloves</i> and his 1890 <i>A Child with Orange</i>, both works late in his life. The American artist of the <a href="/wiki/Ashcan_School" title="Ashcan School">Ashcan School</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Sloan" title="John Sloan">John Sloan</a>, made a 1935 painting <i>Blond Nude with Orange, Blue Couch</i>, while <a href="/wiki/Henri_Matisse" title="Henri Matisse">Henri Matisse</a>'s last painting was his 1951 <i>Nude with Oranges</i>; after that he only made cut-outs.<sup id="cite_ref-Michalska_2023_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michalska_2023-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
